.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_4\_stale_v7_misdispatch\codex_stderr.log:585:outputs/full_scale_v7/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm\report.md:17:Meta-analyses of randomized controlled trials demonstrate a dose-dependent relationship for tirzepatide's effects on HbA1c and body weight in type 2 diabetes.[17] Another meta-analysis confirmed these trends, finding HbA1c reductions of 0.20% with 10 mg and 0.30% with 15 mg versus 5 mg, and weight reductions of 2.64 kg and 4.26 kg, respectively.[18] A more recent network meta-analysis also found significant reductions in HbA1c with tirzepatide 10 mg (MD: -0.19) and 15 mg (MD: -0.32) versus the 5 mg dose, alongside weight reductions of 1.96 kg and 3.31 kg, respectively.[19] Regarding the time to achieve clinical thresholds, a pre-planned exploratory analysis of the SURPASS-2 and SURPASS-3 trials found the median time to first achieve a body weight loss of ≥5% was faster with tirzepatide 5 mg (16.0 weeks) and with the 10 and 15 mg doses (12.4 weeks) than with the active comparator semaglutide 1 mg (24.0 weeks).[20] The same analysis reported the median time to achieve HbA1c <7.0% was 8.1 weeks for each tirzepatide dose.[20] In terms of safety, meta-analyses indicate the risk of total adverse events and gastrointestinal adverse events is higher with the 10 mg and 15 mg doses compared to the 5 mg dose.[17][18] One review concluded that within the 5–15 mg range, the effect on lowering glycemia and reducing weight is dose-dependent, with the 15 mg dose achieving the best efficacy, but the 5 mg dose offers a more favorable safety profile.[17]

.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:225:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:1:# Research report: What is the efficacy and safety of tirzepatide for glycemic control and weight loss in adults with type 2 diabetes?
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:226:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:3:### Efficacy
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:227:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:5:In the SURPASS-1 trial of patients with type 2 diabetes (T2D) inadequately controlled by diet and exercise alone, tirzepatide at 5, 10, and 15 mg doses reduced HbA1c by −1.87%, −1.89%, and −2.07% from baseline versus a 0.04% increase with placebo at 40 weeks.[1][2] Tirzepatide also demonstrated superior efficacy versus basal insulins; in SURPASS-3, it reduced HbA1c by −1.93% to −2.37% across doses versus −1.34% with insulin degludec over 52 weeks, and in SURPASS-4, it reduced HbA1c by −2.24% to −2.58% versus −1.44% with insulin glargine.[1][3][4] For patients on basal insulin with inadequate control, adding tirzepatide in SURPASS-6 was more effective than adding prandial insulin lispro, reducing HbA1c by −2.1% versus −1.1% and reducing body weight by −6.7 to −11.0 kg versus a 3.2 kg gain.[1][5] A post-hoc analysis of SURPASS-2 found tirzepatide was superior to semaglutide 1 mg in achieving composite therapeutic targets, with 57% of patients on tirzepatide 15 mg meeting three or more standard targets (HbA1c <7.0%, weight loss >5%, systolic blood pressure <130 mmHg) versus 34% on semaglutide.[6] Efficacy was consistent across key subgroups; in SURPASS-AP-Combo, greater HbA1c reductions were seen in patients with higher baseline HbA1c (>8.5%), and greater weight loss was seen in those with higher baseline body weight (≥75 kg) across all tirzepatide doses.[7] A pre-planned analysis showed tirzepatide enabled faster achievement of glycemic and weight loss thresholds than semaglutide 1 mg or insulin degludec, with a median time to reach HbA1c <7.0% of 8.1 weeks versus 12.0 and 12.1 weeks, respectively.[8]
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:228:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:7:### Safety
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:229:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:9:Gastrointestinal adverse events were the most commonly reported adverse events with tirzepatide across phase 3 trials in type 2 diabetes.[1][9] In the SURPASS-AP-Combo trial in an Asia-Pacific population, gastrointestinal adverse event incidence was generally similar across subgroups defined by age, sex, baseline HbA1c, BMI, body weight, diabetes duration, and concomitant oral medications.[7] A systematic review and meta-analysis of nine RCTs concluded the gastrointestinal adverse event profile of tirzepatide was consistent with GLP-1 receptor agonists, with incidences of nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea higher than placebo but similar to active comparators like semaglutide and dulaglutide.[10] A systematic review and meta-analysis concluded tirzepatide did not increase events of blood glucose <54 mg/dL or <70 mg/dL compared to control overall.[11] However, when tirzepatide is added to insulin or sulfonylureas, the risk increases; in SURPASS-AP-Combo, where patients were on metformin with or without a sulfonylurea, the incidence of hypoglycemia (blood glucose ≤70 mg/dL or severe) was higher in patients using a concomitant sulfonylurea (e.g., 43.1% with tirzepatide 5 mg) compared to those on metformin alone (6.6% with tirzepatide 5 mg).[7] One meta-analysis specifically noted tirzepatide 15 mg was associated with more hypoglycemia than GLP-1 receptor agonists (pooled RR=3.83), while another network meta-analysis found tirzepatide showed a higher severe hypoglycemia risk compared to semaglutide and liraglutide.[10][12] In clinical trials, no cases of medullary thyroid carcinoma (MTC) were reported; for instance, the SURMOUNT-4 trial specifically stated no cases of medullary thyroid carcinoma or pancreatic cancer were reported.[13] However, when compared to GLP-1 receptor agonists, tirzepatide exhibited a similar risk of medullary thyroid cancer.[14] A 2026 evidence review summarizing multiple large database studies concluded there is no evidence of increased thyroid cancer risk with GLP-1 receptor agonist use, including tirzepatide, over 1.8-3.0 years of follow-up.[15] Furthermore, a review noted that pivotal cardiovascular and obesity outcome trials, including the SURPASS program, did not report clinically meaningful increases in autoimmune thyroid disease or sustained thyroid dysfunction.[16]
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:230:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:11:### Comparative
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:231:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:13:A systematic review and network meta-analysis of subcutaneous GLP-1 receptor agonists and tirzepatide found that all tirzepatide doses were comparable to semaglutide 2.0 mg and superior to semaglutide 1.0 mg and 0.5 mg in reducing HbA1c, with tirzepatide 15 mg ranked as the most efficacious treatment.[17] Tirzepatide also enabled more patients to achieve stringent glycemic targets faster than semaglutide 1 mg, with a median time to achieve HbA1c <7.0% of 8.1 weeks versus 12.0 weeks and to achieve HbA1c ≤6.5% of 12.1 weeks versus 15.7 weeks.[8] A meta-analysis of direct comparative studies in overweight and obese adults, which included populations with and without type 2 diabetes, found tirzepatide was superior to semaglutide for weight reduction, with a standardized mean difference of 0.75 (95% CI 0.52 to 0.92) and greater odds of achieving ≥10% weight loss (OR 0.21, 95% CI 0.06 to 0.78).[18] A post-hoc analysis of SURPASS-2 reported that tirzepatide increased the odds of achieving an intensive weight loss target of >15% compared to semaglutide (OR 3.86, 95% CI 2.69 to 5.55).[6] In SURPASS-3 versus insulin degludec, tirzepatide 5, 10, and 15 mg produced greater HbA1c reductions and significant weight loss, while insulin degludec was associated with weight gain.[3] Furthermore, a network meta-analysis in patients on basal insulin background therapy found tirzepatide 5, 10, and 15 mg provided statistically significant greater reductions in HbA1c and body weight compared to the GLP-1 RAs dulaglutide, exenatide, and lixisenatide.[19] The incidence of hypoglycemia (blood glucose <54 mg/dL) was low and similar between tirzepatide and semaglutide 1 mg.[20] The network meta-analysis comparing subcutaneous agents found both tirzepatide and semaglutide increased the incidence of gastrointestinal adverse events compared to placebo, with no increased risk of serious adverse events or severe hypoglycemia for either.[17]
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:232:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:15:### Dose Response
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:233:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:17:Tirzepatide demonstrates a clear dose-dependent effect on both glycemic control and weight reduction in patients with type 2 diabetes.[1][21][22] Another meta-analysis concluded that within the 5–15 mg range, the effect on lowering glycemia and reducing weight is dose-dependent, with tirzepatide 15 mg achieving the best efficacy.[21] The time to achieve glycemic and weight loss targets is also dose-responsive and faster with higher tirzepatide doses compared to active comparators.[8] A pre-planned analysis of SURPASS-2 and SURPASS-3 found the median time to first achieve HbA1c <7.0% was 8.1 weeks for all tirzepatide doses, compared to 12.0 weeks for semaglutide 1 mg and 12.1 weeks for insulin degludec.[8] Furthermore, the median time to achieve ≥5% weight loss in SURPASS-2 was 16.0 weeks with tirzepatide 5 mg and 12.4 weeks with the 10 mg and 15 mg doses, compared to 24.0 weeks with semaglutide 1 mg.[8] Subgroup analyses indicate that the efficacy of tirzepatide is generally consistent across different patient demographics, though some variations exist.[7] In the SURPASS-AP-Combo trial, patients with a higher baseline HbA1c (>8.5%) experienced significantly greater HbA1c reductions with all tirzepatide doses, while patients with a higher baseline body weight (≥75 kg) experienced significantly greater weight reductions across all doses.[7] An indirect treatment comparison estimated that tirzepatide 10 mg and 15 mg provided significantly greater HbA1c and body weight reductions compared to semaglutide 2.4 mg (an obesity dose in a related population), while tirzepatide 5 mg showed similar efficacy to semaglutide 2.4 mg.[23]
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:234:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:19:### Regulatory
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:235:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:21:Food and Drug Administration (FDA) approved tirzepatide under the trade name Mounjaro in 2022 as an adjunct to diet and exercise to improve glycemic control in adults with type 2 diabetes mellitus.[24][25] The FDA-approved dosing regimen starts at 2.5 mg once weekly, with increases in 2.5 mg increments after a minimum of 4 weeks on the current dose, up to a maximum of 15 mg once weekly.[24][25] The FDA label carries a boxed warning for risk of thyroid C-cell tumors, based on findings in rats, and contraindicates tirzepatide in patients with a personal or family history of medullary thyroid carcinoma or Multiple Endocrine Neoplasia syndrome type 2.[26] The FDA later approved tirzepatide for chronic weight management under the trade name Zepbound in 2023, indicated as an adjunct to a reduced-calorie diet and increased physical activity for adults with obesity (BMI ≥30 kg/m²) or overweight (BMI ≥27 kg/m²) with at least one weight-related comorbid condition.[27][28] The European Medicines Agency (EMA) granted a marketing authorization for Mounjaro valid throughout the EU on 15 September 2022.[29] The EMA's approved indication for type 2 diabetes is for the treatment of adults, adolescents, and children aged 10 years and above with insufficiently controlled disease as an adjunct to diet and exercise, as monotherapy when metformin is inappropriate or in addition to other antidiabetic products.[30] The EMA also approved an indication for weight management, defined as an adjunct to a reduced-calorie diet and increased physical activity for weight management in adults with an initial BMI of ≥30 kg/m² (obesity) or ≥27 kg/m² to <30 kg/m² (overweight) in the presence of at least one weight-related comorbidity.[31][30][32] The UK's National Institute for Health and Care Excellence (NICE) recommends tirzepatide for treating type 2 diabetes in adults only under specific criteria: when triple therapy with metformin and two other oral drugs is ineffective, not tolerated, or contraindicated, and the patient has a BMI of 35 kg/m² or more with specific problems associated with obesity, or a BMI below 35 kg/m² where insulin therapy has significant occupational implications or weight loss would benefit other significant obesity-related complications.[33][34] NICE's recommendation is contingent upon a commercial arrangement that makes tirzepatide available to the NHS at a cost-effective price.[33][34] For weight management, NICE recommends tirzepatide as an option for people with a BMI of more than 35 and at least one weight-related illness, with an initial rollout prioritized for those in specialist services.[35] Key labeling warnings and precautions are consistent across authorities and include risks of severe gastrointestinal adverse reactions, acute pancreatitis, acute kidney injury, acute gallbladder disease, hypersensitivity reactions, and hypoglycemia with concomitant insulin or insulin secretagogues.[36][26][30] The FDA and EMA labels also warn that tirzepatide delays gastric emptying, which can impact the absorption of concomitantly administered oral medications, necessitating guidance for patients using oral contraceptives.[25][37][30] The Canadian Product Monograph for Zepbound similarly contraindicates use in patients with a personal or family history of medullary thyroid carcinoma and warns of gastrointestinal adverse reactions and the potential for drug interactions due to delayed gastric emptying.[38]

.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:3487:bib=json.loads(pathlib.Path('outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/bibliography.json').read_text(encoding='utf-8'))
.\outputs\codex_findings\dr_output_pass_10\codex_stderr.log:3566:outputs/full_scale_v21/clinical/clinical_tirzepatide_t2dm/report.md:21:Food and Drug Administration (FDA) approved tirzepatide under the trade name Mounjaro in 2022 as an adjunct to diet and exercise to improve glycemic control in adults with type 2 diabetes mellitus.[24][25] The FDA-approved dosing regimen starts at 2.5 mg once weekly, with increases in 2.5 mg increments after a minimum of 4 weeks on the current dose, up to a maximum of 15 mg once weekly.[24][25] The FDA label carries a boxed warning for risk of thyroid C-cell tumors, based on findings in rats, and contraindicates tirzepatide in patients with a personal or family history of medullary thyroid carcinoma or Multiple Endocrine Neoplasia syndrome type 2.[26] The FDA later approved tirzepatide for chronic weight management under the trade name Zepbound in 2023, indicated as an adjunct to a reduced-calorie diet and increased physical activity for adults with obesity (BMI ≥30 kg/m²) or overweight (BMI ≥27 kg/m²) with at least one weight-related comorbid condition.[27][28] The European Medicines Agency (EMA) granted a marketing authorization for Mounjaro valid throughout the EU on 15 September 2022.[29] The EMA's approved indication for type 2 diabetes is for the treatment of adults, adolescents, and children aged 10 years and above with insufficiently controlled disease as an adjunct to diet and exercise, as monotherapy when metformin is inappropriate or in addition to other antidiabetic products.[30] The EMA also approved an indication for weight management, defined as an adjunct to a reduced-calorie diet and increased physical activity for weight management in adults with an initial BMI of ≥30 kg/m² (obesity) or ≥27 kg/m² to <30 kg/m² (overweight) in the presence of at least one weight-related comorbidity.[31][30][32] The UK's National Institute for Health and Care Excellence (NICE) recommends tirzepatide for treating type 2 diabetes in adults only under specific criteria: when triple therapy with metformin and two other oral drugs is ineffective, not tolerated, or contraindicated, and the patient has a BMI of 35 kg/m² or more with specific problems associated with obesity, or a BMI below 35 kg/m² where insulin therapy has significant occupational implications or weight loss would benefit other significant obesity-related complications.[33][34] NICE's recommendation is contingent upon a commercial arrangement that makes tirzepatide available to the NHS at a cost-effective price.[33][34] For weight management, NICE recommends tirzepatide as an option for people with a BMI of more than 35 and at least one weight-related illness, with an initial rollout prioritized for those in specialist services.[35] Key labeling warnings and precautions are consistent across authorities and include risks of severe gastrointestinal adverse reactions, acute pancreatitis, acute kidney injury, acute gallbladder disease, hypersensitivity reactions, and hypoglycemia with concomitant insulin or insulin secretagogues.[36][26][30] The FDA and EMA labels also warn that tirzepatide delays gastric emptying, which can impact the absorption of concomitantly administered oral medications, necessitating guidance for patients using oral contraceptives.[25][37][30] The Canadian Product Monograph for Zepbound similarly contraindicates use in patients with a personal or family history of medullary thyroid carcinoma and warns of gastrointestinal adverse reactions and the potential for drug interactions due to delayed gastric emptying.[38]

  620: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------

  622: @dataclass
  623: class SectionValidationResult:
  624:     section_id: str
  625:     section_title: str
  626:     original_text: str
  627:     rendered_text: str
  628:     gap_records: list[GapRecord] = field(default_factory=list)
  629: 
  630:     @property
  631:     def refusal_count(self) -> int:
  632:         return sum(1 for g in self.gap_records if g.action == RefusalAction.REFUSED)
  633: 
  634:     @property
  635:     def soft_mismatch_count(self) -> int:
  636:         return sum(1 for g in self.gap_records if g.action == RefusalAction.LOGGED_ONLY)
  637: 
  638:     @property
  639:     def allowed_count(self) -> int:
  640:         return sum(1 for g in self.gap_records if g.action == RefusalAction.ALLOWED)

  643: def validate_section(
  644:     section_text: str,
  645:     section_id: str,
  646:     section_title: str,
  647:     catalog: dict[str, ClaimAtom],
  648: ) -> SectionValidationResult:
  649:     """Validate all sentences in a section, preserving paragraph
  650:     structure. Sentence-level refusal: refused sentences are replaced
  651:     in `rendered_text`, others kept as-is.
  652: 
  653:     Step 3b commit 2 (Codex iter-2 P2.4): split on paragraph boundaries
  654:     FIRST, validate per paragraph, join with \\n\\n. Sentence_index is
  655:     MONOTONIC across paragraphs so gaps.json claim_id values stay
  656:     unique. Previously a single " ".join collapsed all paragraphs into
  657:     a single line — broke report.md formatting.
  658:     """
  659:     if not section_text or not section_text.strip():
  660:         return SectionValidationResult(
  661:             section_id=section_id,
  662:             section_title=section_title,
  663:             original_text=section_text,
  664:             rendered_text=section_text,
  665:             gap_records=[],
  666:         )
  667: 
  668:     paragraphs = re.split(r"\n{2,}", section_text)
  669:     rendered_paragraphs: list[str] = []
  670:     gap_records: list[GapRecord] = []
  671:     sentence_index = 0  # monotonic across paragraphs
  672: 
  673:     for para in paragraphs:
  674:         para_stripped = para.strip()
  675:         if not para_stripped:
  676:             rendered_paragraphs.append(para)  # preserve whitespace-only paragraph
  677:             continue
  678:         sentences = split_sentences(para_stripped)
  679:         rendered_sentences: list[str] = []
  680:         for sent in sentences:
  681:             record = validate_sentence(
  682:                 sent, sentence_index, section_id, section_title, catalog,
  683:             )
  684:             gap_records.append(record)
  685:             rendered_sentences.append(record.rendered_text)
  686:             sentence_index += 1
  687:         rendered_paragraphs.append(" ".join(rendered_sentences))
  688: 
  689:     rendered_text = "\n\n".join(rendered_paragraphs)
  690:     return SectionValidationResult(
  691:         section_id=section_id,
  692:         section_title=section_title,
  693:         original_text=section_text,
  694:         rendered_text=rendered_text,
  695:         gap_records=gap_records,
  696:     )
  697: 
  698: 
  699: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
  700: # gaps.json sidecar writer
  701: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
  702: 
  703: def build_gaps_document(
  704:     document_id: str,
  705:     section_results: list[SectionValidationResult],
  706: ) -> dict:
  707:     """Per Codex APPROVE_DESIGN gaps.json schema."""
  708:     return {
  709:         "document_id": document_id,
  710:         "generated_at": datetime.now(tz=timezone.utc).isoformat(),
  711:         "sections": [
  712:             {
  713:                 "section_id": s.section_id,
  714:                 "section_title": s.section_title,
  715:                 "claims": [g.to_dict() for g in s.gap_records],
  716:                 "summary": {
  717:                     "total_sentences": len(s.gap_records),
  718:                     "refused": s.refusal_count,
  719:                     "soft_mismatch": s.soft_mismatch_count,
  720:                     "allowed": s.allowed_count,
  721:                 },
  722:             }
  723:             for s in section_results
  724:         ],
  725:         "totals": {
  726:             "total_sentences": sum(len(s.gap_records) for s in section_results),
  727:             "refused": sum(s.refusal_count for s in section_results),
  728:             "soft_mismatch": sum(s.soft_mismatch_count for s in section_results),
  729:             "allowed": sum(s.allowed_count for s in section_results),
  730:         },
  731:     }
  732: 
  733: 
  734: def write_gaps_sidecar(
  735:     output_dir: Path,
  736:     document_id: str,
  737:     section_results: list[SectionValidationResult],
  738:     *,
  739:     filename: str = "gaps.json",
  740: ) -> Path:
  741:     """Write the gaps.json sidecar next to report.md.
  742: 
  743:     Returns the written path. Caller is responsible for ensuring
  744:     `output_dir` exists.
  745:     """
  746:     doc = build_gaps_document(document_id, section_results)
  747:     path = output_dir / filename
  748:     path.write_text(
  749:         json.dumps(doc, indent=2, ensure_ascii=False),
  750:         encoding="utf-8",
  751:     )
  752:     return path

    3: Per Codex APPROVE_DESIGN 2026-05-26 (refusal_design_verdict):

    5:     - STRICT layer: missing/invalid atom_id → REPLACE sentence with refusal
    6:     - SOFT layer: value/endpoint/entity/timepoint mismatch → log only, do
    7:       NOT replace (during demo period)

   34:     format_refusal_for_missing_atom,
   35: )
   36: 
   37: 
   38: class RefusalAction(str, Enum):
   39:     REFUSED = "refused"            # sentence replaced with refusal template
   40:     ALLOWED = "allowed"            # sentence kept as-is (atom valid)
   41:     LOGGED_ONLY = "logged_only"    # sentence kept but soft mismatch logged
   42: 
   43: 
   44: class RefusalReason(str, Enum):
   45:     MISSING_ATOM_CITATION = "missing_atom_citation"   # no atom_NNN in sentence
   46:     INVALID_ATOM_ID = "invalid_atom_id"               # atom_NNN cited but not in catalog
   47:     EV_CITATION_FOR_CLAIM = "ev_citation_for_claim"   # [ev_XXX] used for factual claim
   48:     SOFT_MISMATCH = "soft_mismatch"                   # cited atom value/endpoint differs
   49:     PARTIAL_COVERAGE_SUSPECTED = "partial_coverage_suspected"
   50:     NO_VIOLATION = "no_violation"                     # sentence is valid

   52: 
   53: # ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
   54: # Quantitative-claim detector (per Codex APPROVE_DESIGN trigger schema)

   60: # Numbers in sentence — require non-letter context to avoid matching
   61: # embedded digits in endpoint names like "HbA1c" (the "1") or "T2DM"
   62: # (the "2"). Same approach as atom_extractor._NUMBER_ATOM_RE.
   63: _NUMBER_RE = re.compile(
   64:     r"(?<![A-Za-z0-9_.])[-−]?\d+(?:\.\d+)?(?![A-Za-z0-9_])"
   65: )
   66: 
   67: # Endpoint vocab — superset for claim-detection (any one of these triggers
   68: # atom-citation requirement when combined with a number or with a
   69: # qualitative comparative term)
   70: _ENDPOINT_VOCAB_RE = re.compile(
   71:     r"\b("
   72:     r"hba1c|fasting\s+(?:plasma\s+)?glucose|fpg|fsg|"
   73:     r"body\s+weight|weight\s+(?:loss|reduction)|bmi|waist\s+circumference|"
   74:     r"ldl[-\s]?c|hdl[-\s]?c|triglycerides|cholesterol|"
   75:     r"blood\s+pressure|systolic|diastolic|sbp|dbp|mmhg|"
   76:     r"mace|myocardial\s+infarction|stroke|cv\s+death|cardiovascular\s+death|"
   77:     r"all[-\s]?cause\s+mortality|heart\s+failure|hf\s+hospitali[zs]ation|"
   78:     r"egfr|uacr|aki|creatinine|"
   79:     r"adverse\s+events?|aes?|serious\s+adverse|sae|discontinuation|"
   80:     # Iter-2 fix (Codex iter-1 P1): common safety endpoints by name
   81:     r"nausea|vomiting|diarrh(?:o)?ea|constipation|abdominal\s+pain|"
   82:     r"injection[-\s]?site\s+reaction|"
   83:     r"hypoglycemia|hypoglycaemia|pancreatitis|gallbladder|retinopathy|mtc|"
   84:     r"hazard\s+ratio|risk\s+ratio|odds\s+ratio|relative\s+risk|"
   85:     r"responder\s+rate|response\s+rate|incidence|"
   86:     r"non[-\s]?inferiority|superior(?:ity)?"
   87:     r")\b",
   88:     re.IGNORECASE,
   89: )
   90: 
   91: # NOTE (iter-4 design decision): two prior iterations (iter-2, iter-3)
   92: # attempted an eligibility-range override via these two regex constants.
   93: # Codex iter-3 caught that pure-regex disambiguation between
   94: # "eligibility framing + endpoint+number" and "outcome claim + baseline
   95: # mention" is fundamentally fragile. Per CLAUDE.md §-1.1 clinical-safety
   96: # principle, the safer default is: any quantitative claim requires
   97: # atom citation. Constants retained for any future revisit but NO
   98: # LONGER WIRED into requires_atom_citation().
   99: _ELIGIBILITY_RANGE_RE = re.compile(
  100:     r"\b(?:inclusion\s+criter\w*|exclusion\s+criter\w*|"
  101:     r"eligibility\s+criter\w*|eligible\s+(?:if|when)\b|"
  102:     r"required\s+(?:hba1c|weight|bmi)\s+(?:of|between)\b)",
  103:     re.IGNORECASE,
  104: )
  105: _OUTCOME_VERB_WITH_NUMBER_RE = re.compile(
  106:     r"\b(?:reduc(?:ed|tions?|ing)|"
  107:     r"decreas(?:ed|es?|ing)|increas(?:ed|es?|ing)|"
  108:     r"chang(?:ed|es?)|improv(?:ed|ements?|ing)|"
  109:     r"lower(?:ed|ing)|rais(?:ed|ing)|"
  110:     r"fell|rose|dropped)\s+"
  111:     r"(?:by|of|from|to)?\s*[-−]?\d",
  112:     re.IGNORECASE,
  113: )
  114: 
  115: # Qualitative comparative/outcome language requiring atom citation
  116: # (Codex iter-1 P1 expanded: catch "more common with X than Y", "higher
  117: # with X than Y", "greater reduction than", etc.)
  118: _QUAL_COMPARATIVE_RE = re.compile(
  119:     r"\b("
  120:     r"greater(?:\s+\w+){0,4}\s+than|"

 succeeded in 784ms:

   10:     The current architecture lets V4 Pro write open prose, then catches
   11:     fabrications post-hoc via strict_verify. Codex's read: that's
   12:     backwards. The fix is to PRE-EXTRACT verifiable facts (atoms) from
   13:     cited evidence spans BEFORE generation, then constrain V4 Pro to
   14:     cite atom_ids that map to fixed spans. V4 Pro becomes the
   15:     rhetorical/synthesis layer, not the fact source.

   18:     P1.1 unit_extraction_corrupts_units (line 209)
   19:         Fix: special-case `%` without word boundary; word units need
   20:         leading boundary; restrict fallback unit-scan to immediate
   21:         post-value or comma-coordinate context only.
   22: 
   23:     P1.2 numeric_anchor_filter_emits_non_claim_values (line 438)
   24:         Fix: NumberRole classifier (OUTCOME / DOSE / TIMEPOINT /
   25:         SAMPLE_SIZE / CI_BOUND / CI_LEVEL) runs BEFORE atom creation.
   26:         Only OUTCOME numbers become atoms. Doses, timepoints, sample
   27:         sizes are stored as metadata fields on the OUTCOME atom they
   28:         modify, not as separate atoms.
   29: 
   30:     P1.3 entity_comparator_binding_flips_arms (line 362)
   31:         Fix: arm-local entity binding. For each value, find the
   32:         nearest arm phrase ("with X", "X mg", "X group") within
   33:         ~50 chars to the right (where clinical text typically places
   34:         the arm label). Comparator from explicit "versus X" /
   35:         "compared to Y" syntax separately.
